<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the economic mechanism. <ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/16px-Emblem-money.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/24px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/32px-Emblem-money.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portal:Business_and_economics" title="Portal:Business and economics">Business and economics portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Business_administration" title="Template:Business administration"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Business_administration" title="Template talk:Business administration"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Business_administration&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Trade</b> involves the transfer of goods and services from one person or entity to another, often in exchange for money. Economists refer to a <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">system</a> or network that allows trade as a <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a>.
</p><p>An early form of trade, <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">barter</a>, saw the direct exchange of goods and services for other goods and services,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samuelson1939_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samuelson1939-1">[1]</a></sup> i.e. trading things without the use of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samuelson1939_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Samuelson1939-1">[1]</a></sup> Modern traders generally negotiate through a medium of exchange, such as money. As a result, <b>buying</b> can be separated from <b>selling</b>, or <a href="/wiki/Earnings" title="Earnings">earning</a>. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_money#Emergence_of_money" title="History of money">invention of money</a> (and letter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credit_(finance)" title="Credit (finance)">credit</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">paper money</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Digital_currency" title="Digital currency">non-physical money</a>) greatly simplified and promoted trade.  Trade between two traders is called <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_trade" title="Bilateral trade">bilateral trade</a>, while trade involving more than two traders is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multilateral_trade" title="Multilateral trade">multilateral trade</a>.
</p><p>In one modern view, trade exists due to specialization and the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a>, a predominant form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_activity" title="Economic activity">economic activity</a> in which individuals and groups concentrate on a small aspect of production, but use their output in trades for other products and needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DollarKraay2004_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-DollarKraay2004-2">[2]</a></sup> Trade exists between regions because different regions may have a <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a> (perceived or real) in the production of some trade-able <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a>—including production of natural resources scarce or limited elsewhere. For example: different regions' sizes may encourage <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a>. In such circumstances, trade at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_price" title="Market price">market prices</a> between locations can benefit both locations. Different types of traders may specialize in trading different kinds of goods; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grain_trade" title="Grain trade">grain trade</a> have both historically been important in the development of a global, international economy.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Retail" title="Retail">Retail</a> trade consists of the <a href="/wiki/Sales" title="Sales">sale</a> of goods or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchandise" title="Merchandise">merchandise</a> from a very fixed location<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> (such as a <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department store</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boutique" title="Boutique">boutique</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kiosk" title="Kiosk">kiosk</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Online" title="Online">online</a> or by <a href="/wiki/Mail" title="Mail">mail</a>, in small or individual lots for direct <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> or use by the purchaser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fas_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-fas-4">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wholesale" title="Wholesale">Wholesale</a> trade is traffic in goods that are sold as merchandise to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retailer" title="Retailer">retailers</a>, or to industrial, commercial, institutional, or other professional <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">business</a> users, or to other wholesalers and related subordinated services.
</p><p>Historically, openness to <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> substantially increased in some areas from 1815 to the outbreak of World War I in 1914. Trade openness increased again during the 1920s but collapsed (in particular in Europe and North America) during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s. Trade openness increased substantially again from the 1950s onwards (albeit with a slowdown during the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil crisis of the 1970s</a>). Economists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_historians" title="Economic historians">economic historians</a> contend that current levels of trade openness are the highest they have ever been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Trade</i> is from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_English_language" title="Middle English language">Middle English</a> <i>trade</i> ("path, course of conduct"), introduced into English by Hanseatic merchants, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German_language" title="Middle Low German language">Middle Low German</a> <i>trade</i> ("track, course"), from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Saxon_language" title="Old Saxon language">Old Saxon</a> <i>trada</i> ("spoor, track"), from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*tradō</i> ("track, way"), and cognate with <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>tredan</i> ("to tread").
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">Commerce</a></i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>commercium</i>, from <i>cum</i> "together" and <i>merx</i>, "merchandise."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_world" title="Economic history of the world">Economic history of the world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_international_trade" title="Timeline of international trade">Timeline of international trade</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Trade originated from <a href="/wiki/History_of_communication" title="History of communication">human communication</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> times. Trading was the main facility of prehistoric people,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> who exchanged goods and services from each other in a gift economy before the innovation of modern-day currency. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Watson_(business_writer)" title="Peter Watson (business writer)">Peter Watson</a> dates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_international_trade" title="History of international trade">history of long-distance commerce</a> from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 150,000</span> years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson2005_150_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2005_150-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Mediterranean region, the earliest contact between cultures involved members of the species <i>Homo sapiens</i>, principally using the Danube river, at a time beginning 35,000–30,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abulafia,_Rackham,_Suano_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abulafia,_Rackham,_Suano-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (September 2019)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2019)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> trace the origins of commerce to the very start of <a href="/wiki/Financial_transaction" title="Financial transaction">transactions</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> times. Apart from traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a>, trading became a principal <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Faculty_(disambiguation)" title="Faculty (disambiguation)">facility</a> of prehistoric people, who <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">bartered</a> what they had for goods and services from each other.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caduceus.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Caduceus.svg/100px-Caduceus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Caduceus.svg/150px-Caduceus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Caduceus.svg/200px-Caduceus.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caduceus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Caduceus" title="Caduceus">caduceus</a>, traditionally associated which <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a> (the Roman patron-god of merchants), continues in use as a symbol of commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_history">Ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2280" data-file-width="3248" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg/220px-Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg/330px-Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg/440px-Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attic_red-figure_Pottery_in_the_Eremitage_Sankt_Petersburg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Etruscan_art" title="Etruscan art">Ancient Etruscan</a> "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aryballoi" title="Aryballoi">aryballoi</a>" terracota vessels unearthed in the 1860s at Bolshaya Bliznitsa tumulus near <a href="/wiki/Phanagoria" title="Phanagoria">Phanagoria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Russia" title="Southern Russia">South Russia</a> (formerly part of the <a href="/wiki/Bosporan_Kingdom" title="Bosporan Kingdom">Bosporan Kingdom</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimmerian_Bosporus" title="Cimmerian Bosporus">Cimmerian Bosporus</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Taman_Peninsula" title="Taman Peninsula">Taman Peninsula</a>); on exhibit at the <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a></div></div></div>
<p>Trade is believed to have taken place throughout much of recorded human history. There is evidence of the exchange of <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. Trade in obsidian is believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to have taken place in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> from 17,000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The earliest use of obsidian in the Near East dates to the Lower and Middle paleolithic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Takahito,_Prince_Mikasa" title="Takahito, Prince Mikasa">HIH Prince Mikasa no Miya Takahito</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Carr_Bosanquet" title="Robert Carr Bosanquet">Robert Carr Bosanquet</a> investigated trade in the Stone Age by excavations in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Trade is believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to have first begun in south west Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeological evidence of obsidian use provides data on how this material was increasingly the preferred choice rather than <a href="/wiki/Chert" title="Chert">chert</a> from the late Mesolithic to Neolithic, requiring exchange as deposits of obsidian are rare in the Mediterranean region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Robb_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Robb-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Obsidian is thought<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to have provided the material to make cutting utensils or tools, although since other more easily obtainable materials were available, use was found<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> exclusive to the higher status of the tribe using  "the rich man's flint".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Interestingly, Obsidian has held its value relative to flint.
</p><p>Early traders traded Obsidian at distances of 900 kilometres within the Mediterranean region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade in the Mediterranean during the Neolithic of Europe was greatest in this material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Robb_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Robb-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Networks were in existence at around 12,000 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrea_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrea-28">[28]</a></sup> Anatolia was the source primarily for trade with the Levant, Iran and Egypt according to Zarins study of 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pliny_the_Elder_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pliny_the_Elder-31">[31]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melos" title="Melos">Melos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipari" title="Lipari">Lipari</a> sources produced among the most widespread trading in the Mediterranean region as known to archaeology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_Blake,_A_B_Knapp_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-E_Blake,_A_B_Knapp-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sar-i_Sang" title="Sar-i Sang">Sari-i-Sang</a> mine in the mountains of Afghanistan was the largest source for trade of <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The material was most largely traded during the <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassite period</a> of Babylonia beginning 1595 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_trade">Later trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Later trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediterranean_and_Near_East">Mediterranean and Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mediterranean and Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a> was a prominent trading center during the third millennia BCE, with a network reaching into Anatolia and north Mesopotamia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_Blake,_A_B_Knapp_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-E_Blake,_A_B_Knapp-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laura_S._Etheredge_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laura_S._Etheredge-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumper_&amp;_Stanley_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dumper_&amp;_Stanley-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2868" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/220px-Silk_route.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/330px-Silk_route.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/440px-Silk_route.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> trade route between Europe and Asia</div></div></div>
<p>Materials used for creating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewelry" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a> were traded with Egypt since 3000 BCE. Long-range trade routes first appeared in the 3rd millennium BCE, when <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> traded with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harappan_civilization" title="Harappan civilization">Harappan civilization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus Valley</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> were noted sea traders, traveling across the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, and as far north as <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Britain</a> for sources of <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>. For this purpose they established trade colonies the Greeks called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emporia_(ancient_Greece)" title="Emporia (ancient Greece)">emporia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archa_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-archa-40">[40]</a></sup> Along the coast of the Mediterranean, researchers have found a positive relationship between how well-connected a coastal location was and the local prevalence of archaeological sites from the Iron Age. This suggests that a location's trade potential was an important determinant of human settlements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the beginning of Greek <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the 5th century, a financially lucrative trade brought valuable <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spice</a> to Europe from the far east, including India and China. <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman commerce</a> allowed its empire to flourish and endure. The latter Roman Republic and the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a> of the Roman empire produced a stable and secure transportation network that enabled the shipment of trade goods without fear of significant <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>, as Rome had become the sole effective sea power in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> with the conquest of Egypt and the near east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In ancient Greece <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> was the god of trade<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> (commerce) and weights and measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In ancient Rome, <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercurius</a></i> was the god of merchants, whose festival was celebrated by traders on the 25th day of the fifth month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The concept of free trade was an antithesis to the will and economic direction of the sovereigns of the ancient Greek states. Free trade between states was stifled by the need for strict internal controls (via taxation) to maintain security within the treasury of the sovereign, which nevertheless enabled the maintenance of a <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/modicum" title="wikt:modicum">modicum</a></i> of civility within the structures of functional community life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fall of the Roman empire and the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a> brought instability to <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and a near-collapse of the trade network in the western world. Trade, however, continued to flourish among the kingdoms of Africa, the Middle East, India, China, and Southeast Asia. Some trade did occur in the west. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Radhanite" title="Radhanite">Radhanites</a> were a medieval guild or group (the precise meaning of the word is lost to history) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jewish</a> merchants who traded between the <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> in Europe and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> of the Near East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Indo-Pacific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_Jade_Road" title="Maritime Jade Road">Maritime Jade Road</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">Maritime Silk Road</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="2895" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/220px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/330px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png/440px-Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austronesian_maritime_trade_network_in_the_Indian_Ocean.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">proto-historic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">historic</a> maritime trade network in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manguin2016_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manguin2016-51">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The first true maritime trade network in the Indian Ocean was by the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manguin2016_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manguin2016-51">[51]</a></sup> Initiated by the animist indigenous peoples of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_Jade_Road" title="Maritime Jade Road">Maritime Jade Road</a> was an extensive trading network connecting multiple areas in Southeast and East Asia. Its primary products were made of jade mined from Taiwan by animist <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_indigenous_peoples" title="Taiwanese indigenous peoples">Taiwanese indigenous peoples</a> and processed mostly in the Philippines by animist indigenous Filipinos, especially in <a href="/wiki/Batanes" title="Batanes">Batanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palawan" title="Palawan">Palawan</a>. Some were also processed in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, while the peoples of <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> also participated in the massive animist-led trading network. Participants in the network at the time had a majority animist population. The maritime road is one of the most extensive sea-based trade networks of a single geological material in the prehistoric world. It was in existence for at least 3,000 years, where its peak production was from 2000 BCE to 500 CE, older than the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> in mainland Eurasia and the later <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">Maritime Silk Road</a>. The Maritime Jade Road began to wane during its final centuries from 500 CE until 1000 CE. The entire period of the network was a golden age for the diverse animist societies of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea-faring Southeast Asians also established trade routes with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">Southern India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> as early as 1500 BC, ushering an exchange of material culture (like <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">catamarans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outrigger_boat" title="Outrigger boat">outrigger boats</a>, sewn-plank boats, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paan" title="Paan">paan</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Cultigen" title="Cultigen">cultigens</a> (like <a href="/wiki/Coconut" title="Coconut">coconuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">sandalwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">bananas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>); as well as connecting the material cultures of India and China. <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Indonesia" title="Ethnic groups in Indonesia">Indonesians</a>, in particular were trading in spices (mainly <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">cinnamon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassia_bark" title="Cassia bark">cassia</a>) with <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> using <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">catamaran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outrigger_boat" title="Outrigger boat">outrigger boats</a> and sailing with the help of the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">Westerlies</a> in the Indian Ocean. This trade network expanded to reach as far as Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, resulting in the Austronesian colonization of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> by the first half of the first millennium AD. It continued up to historic times, later becoming the Maritime Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manguin2016_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Manguin2016-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1974_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1974-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1981_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1981-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlenchFruits_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlenchFruits-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mesoamerica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_(siglos_XIV-XV).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4638" data-file-width="5675" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg/220px-Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg/330px-Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg/440px-Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_%28siglos_XIV-XV%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinero_azada_de_Mexico_(siglos_XIV-XV).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Tajadero</i> or <a href="/wiki/Axe-monies" title="Axe-monies">axe money</a> used as currency in <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>. It had a fixed worth of 8,000 <a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">cacao</a> seeds, which were also used as currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The emergence of exchange networks in the Pre-Columbian societies of and near to Mexico are known to have occurred within recent years before and after 1500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade networks reached north to <a href="/wiki/Oasisamerica" title="Oasisamerica">Oasisamerica</a>. There is evidence of established maritime trade with the cultures of northwestern South America and the Caribbean.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, commerce developed in Europe by trading luxury goods at trade fairs. Wealth became converted into movable wealth or <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a>. Banking systems developed where money on account was transferred across national boundaries. Hand to hand markets became a feature of town life and were regulated by town authorities.
</p><p>
Western Europe established a complex and expansive trade network with cargo ships being the main carrier of goods; <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">Cogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(ship_type)" title="Hulk (ship type)">Hulks</a> are two examples of such cargo ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Many ports would develop their own extensive trade networks. The English port city of <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> traded with peoples from what is modern day Iceland, all along the western coast of France, and down to what is now Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1457" decoding="async" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg/450px-Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg/675px-Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg/900px-Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Late_Medieval_Trade_Routes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map showing the main <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> for goods within <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">late medieval Europe</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the Middle Ages, Central Asia was the economic center of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beckwith2011_xxiv_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beckwith2011_xxiv-64">[64]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdians</a> dominated the east–west trade route known as the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> after the 4th century CE up to the 8th century CE, with <a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taraz" title="Taraz">Talas</a> ranking among their main centers in the north. They were the main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caravan_(travelers)" title="Caravan (travelers)">caravan</a> merchants of Central Asia.
</p><p>From the Middle Ages, the <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">maritime republics</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Pisa" title="Republic of Pisa">Pisa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a>, played a key role in trade along the Mediterranean. From the 11th to the late 15th centuries, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venetian_Republic" title="Venetian Republic">Venetian Republic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a> were major trade centers. They dominated trade in the Mediterranean and the Black Sea, having the monopoly between Europe and the Near East for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 8th to the 11th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">Varangians</a> traded as they sailed from and to Scandinavia.  Vikings sailed to Western Europe, while Varangians to Russia.  The <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> was an alliance of trading cities that maintained a trade <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> over most of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic</a>, between the 13th and 17th centuries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Age_of_Sail_and_the_Industrial_Revolution">The Age of Sail and the Industrial Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Age of Sail and the Industrial Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Portuguese explorer <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> pioneered the European <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> in 1498 when he reached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Kozhikode" title="History of Kozhikode">Calicut</a> after sailing around the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> at the southern tip of the African continent. Prior to this, the flow of spice into Europe from India was controlled by Islamic powers, especially Egypt. The spice trade was of major economic importance and helped spur the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> in Europe. Spices brought to Europe from the Eastern world were some of the most valuable commodities for their weight, sometimes rivaling <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>.
</p><p>From 1070 onward, kingdoms in West <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> became <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Africa" title="Economic history of Africa">significant members of global trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> This came initially through the movement of gold and other resources sent out by <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> traders on the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trading</a> network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> Beginning in the 16th century, European merchants would purchase gold, spices, cloth, timber and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">slaves</a> from West African states as part of the <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">triangular trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> This was often in exchange for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloth" title="Cloth">cloth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cowrie_shells" title="Cowrie shells">cowrie shells</a> which were used locally as currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Founded in 1352, the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Sultanate" title="Bengal Sultanate">Bengal Sultanate</a> was a major <a href="/wiki/Trading_nation" title="Trading nation">trading nation</a> in the world and often referred to by Europeans as the wealthiest country with which to trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th and 17th centuries, the Portuguese gained an economic advantage in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</a> due to different philosophies of trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup> Whereas Portuguese traders concentrated on the accumulation of capital, in Kongo spiritual meaning was attached to many objects of trade. According to economic historian <a href="/wiki/Toby_Green" title="Toby Green">Toby Green</a>, in Kongo "giving more than receiving was a symbol of spiritual and political power and privilege."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_67-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Seventeen_Provinces" title="Seventeen Provinces">Seventeen Provinces</a> were the center of free trade, imposing no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exchange_control" title="Exchange control">exchange controls</a>, and advocating the free movement of goods.  Trade in the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> was dominated by Portugal in the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> in the 17th century, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> in the 18th century. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> developed regular trade links across both the Atlantic and the Pacific Oceans.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg/250px-Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg/375px-Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg/500px-Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wojciech_Gerson_-_Gda%C5%84sk_in_the_XVII_century.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danzig" title="Danzig">Danzig</a> in the 17th century, a port of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1776, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> published the paper <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/An_Inquiry_into_the_Nature_and_Causes_of_the_Wealth_of_Nations" title="An Inquiry into the Nature and Causes of the Wealth of Nations">An Inquiry into the Nature and Causes of the Wealth of Nations</a></i>. It criticized <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Mercantilism</a>, and argued that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic" title="Economic">economic</a> specialization could benefit nations just as much as firms.  Since the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a> was restricted by the size of the market, he said that countries having access to larger markets would be able to divide labour more efficiently and thereby become more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Productive" title="Productive">productive</a>. Smith said that he considered all rationalizations of <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">import</a> and <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">export</a> controls "dupery", which hurt the trading nation as a whole for the benefit of specific industries.
</p><p>In 1799, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a>, formerly the world's largest company, became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bankrupt" title="Bankrupt">bankrupt</a>, partly due to the rise of competitive free trade.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="1012" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg/250px-Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg/375px-Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg/500px-Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berber_Trade_with_Timbuktu_1300s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> trade with <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a>, 1853</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1817, <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Mill" title="James Mill">James Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Torrens_(economist)" title="Robert Torrens (economist)">Robert Torrens</a> showed that free trade would benefit the industrially weak as well as the strong, in the famous theory of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principles_of_Political_Economy_and_Taxation" title="Principles of Political Economy and Taxation">Principles of Political Economy and Taxation</a> Ricardo advanced the doctrine still considered the most counterintuitive in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>When an inefficient producer sends the merchandise it produces best to a country able to produce it more efficiently, both countries benefit.</i></dd></dl>
<p>The ascendancy of free trade was primarily based on national advantage in the mid 19th century. That is, the calculation made was whether it was in any particular country's self-interest to open its borders to imports.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> proved that a country with monopoly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pricing_power" title="Pricing power">pricing power</a> on the international market could manipulate the <a href="/wiki/Terms_of_trade" title="Terms of trade">terms of trade</a> through maintaining <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a>, and that the response to this might be <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(international_relations)" title="Reciprocity (international relations)">reciprocity</a> in trade policy.  Ricardo and others had suggested this earlier.  This was taken as evidence against the universal doctrine of free trade, as it was believed that more of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">economic surplus</a> of trade would accrue to a country following <i>reciprocal</i>, rather than completely free, trade policies.  This was followed within a few years by the <a href="/wiki/Infant_industry" title="Infant industry">infant industry</a> scenario developed by Mill promoting the theory that the government had the duty to <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protect</a> young industries, although only for a time necessary for them to develop full capacity. This became the policy in many countries attempting to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialize" title="Industrialize">industrialize</a> and out-compete English exporters. <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a> later continued this vein of thought, showing that in a few circumstances tariffs might be beneficial to the host country; but never for the world at large.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> was a major economic recession that ran from 1929 to the late 1930s.  During this period, there was a great drop in trade and other economic indicators.
</p><p>The lack of free trade was considered by many as a principal cause of the depression causing stagnation and inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>  Only during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> did the recession end in the United States. Also during the war, in 1944, 44 countries signed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_Agreement" title="Bretton Woods Agreement">Bretton Woods Agreement</a>, intended to prevent national trade barriers, to avoid depressions.  It set up rules and institutions to regulate the <a href="/wiki/International_political_economy" title="International political economy">international political economy</a>: the International Monetary Fund and the International Bank for Reconstruction and Development (later divided into the World Bank $ Bank for International Settlements).  These organizations became operational in 1946 after enough countries ratified the agreement. In 1947, 23 countries agreed to the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade" title="General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade">General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade</a> to promote free trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> became the world's largest exporter of manufactured goods and services, the biggest export market for around 80 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">Globalization</a></div>
<p>Today, trade is merely a subset within a complex system of <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">companies</a> which try to maximize their profits by offering <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">products</a> and <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a> (which consists both of individuals and other companies) at the lowest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Production_cost" title="Production cost">production cost</a>. A system of <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> has helped to develop the world economy but, in combination with bilateral or multilateral agreements to lower <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> or to achieve <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, has sometimes harmed <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">third-world markets</a> for local products.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_trade">Free trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Free trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">Free trade</a></div>
<p>Free trade advanced further in the late 20th century and early 2000s:
</p>
<ul><li>1992 <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> lifted barriers to internal trade in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" title="Good (economics)">goods</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" title="Labour (economics)">labour</a>.</li>
<li>January 1, 1994 the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> (NAFTA) took effect.</li>
<li>1994 The GATT <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marrakech_Agreement" title="Marrakech Agreement">Marrakech Agreement</a> specified formation of the WTO.</li>
<li>January 1, 1995 <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> was created to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, by mandating mutual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Most_favored_nation" title="Most favored nation">most favored nation</a> trading status between all signatories.</li>
<li>EC was transformed into the European Union, which accomplished the Economic and Monetary Union (EMU) in 2002, through introducing the Euro, and creating this way a real single market between 13 member states as of January 1, 2007.</li>
<li><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Accarias_de_S%C3%A9rionne_-_Int%C3%A9r%C3%AAts_des_nations_de_l%27Europe,_d%C3%A9v%C3%A9lop%C3%A9s_relativement_au_commerce,_1766_-_5790093.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3954" data-file-width="2768" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Accarias_de_S%C3%A9rionne_-_Int%C3%A9r%C3%AAts_des_nations_de_l%27Europe%2C_d%C3%A9v%C3%A9lop%C3%A9s_relativement_au_commerce%2C_1766_-_5790093.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Accarias_de_S%C3%A9rionne_-_Int%C3%A9r%C3%AAts_des_nations_de_l%27Europe%2C_d%C3%A9v%C3%A9lop%C3%A9s_relativement_au_commerce%2C_1766_-_5790093.tif.jpg" <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Perspectives">Perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protectionism">Protectionism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Protectionism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">Protectionism</a></div>
<p>Protectionism is the policy of restraining and discouraging trade between states and contrasts with the policy of free trade.  This policy often takes the form of <a href="/wiki/Tariff" title="Tariff">tariffs</a> and restrictive <a href="/wiki/Import_quota" title="Import quota">quotas</a>.  Protectionist policies were particularly prevalent in the 1930s, between the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> and the onset of World War II.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Islamic teachings encourage trading (and condemn <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a> or <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">interest</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeao-Christian" title="Judeao-Christian">Judeao-Christian</a> teachings do not prohibit trade. They do prohibit fraud and dishonest measures. Historically they forbade charging interest on loans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_money">Development of money</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Development of money">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_money" title="History of money">History of money</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:221px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_denarius_in_silver_(Maximinus)-transparent.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="219" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Roman_denarius_in_silver_%28Maximinus%29-transparent.png" width="219"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_denarius_in_silver_(Maximinus)-transparent.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first instances of money were objects with intrinsic value. This is called <a href="/wiki/Commodity_money" title="Commodity money">commodity money</a> and includes any commonly available commodity that has intrinsic value; historical examples include pigs, rare seashells, whale's teeth, and (often) cattle. In medieval Iraq, bread was used as an early form of money. In the <a href="/wiki/Aztec_Empire" title="Aztec Empire">Aztec Empire</a>, under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Moctezuma_II" title="Moctezuma II">Montezuma</a> cocoa beans became legitimate currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">Currency</a> was introduced as standardised money to facilitate a wider exchange of goods and services.  This first stage of currency, where metals were used to represent stored value, and symbols to represent commodities, formed the basis of trade in the Fertile Crescent for over 1500 years.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Numismatist" title="Numismatist">Numismatists</a> have examples of coins from the earliest large-scale societies, although these were initially unmarked lumps of <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-originsofmoney_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-originsofmoney-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trends">Trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doha_rounds">Doha rounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Doha rounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doha_round" title="Doha round">Doha round</a></div>
<p>The Doha round of World Trade Organization negotiations aimed to lower <a href="/wiki/Trade_barrier" title="Trade barrier">barriers to trade</a> around the world, with a focus on making <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">trade fairer</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>.  Talks have been hung over a divide between the rich <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>, represented by the <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a>, and the major developing countries. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidies" title="Agricultural subsidies">Agricultural subsidies</a> are the most significant issue upon which agreement has been the hardest to negotiate. By contrast, there was much agreement on <a href="/wiki/Trade_facilitation" title="Trade facilitation">trade facilitation</a> and capacity building. The Doha round began in <a href="/wiki/Doha" title="Doha">Doha</a>, Qatar, and negotiations were continued in: <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">Cancún</a>, Mexico; <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, Switzerland; and <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, France, and Hong Kong.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beginning around 1978, the government of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> (PRC) began an experiment in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_reforms_in_China" title="Economic reforms in China">economic reform</a>. In contrast to the previous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">Soviet</a>-style <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrally_planned_economy" title="Centrally planned economy">centrally planned economy</a>, the new measures progressively relaxed restrictions on farming, agricultural distribution and, several years later, urban enterprises and labor. The more market-oriented approach reduced inefficiencies and stimulated private investment, particularly by farmers, which led to increased productivity and output. One feature was the establishment of four (later five) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_Economic_Zone" title="Special Economic Zone">Special Economic Zones</a> located along the South-east coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reforms proved spectacularly successful in terms of increased output, variety, quality, <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demand" title="Demand">demand</a>. In real terms, the economy doubled in size between 1978 and 1986, doubled again by 1994, and again by 2003. On a real per capita basis, doubling from the 1978 base took place in 1987, 1996 and 2006. By 2008, the economy was 16.7 times the size it was in 1978, and 12.1 times its previous per capita levels. International trade progressed even more rapidly, doubling on average every 4.5 years. Total two-way trade in January 1998 exceeded that for all of 1978; in the first quarter of 2009, trade exceeded the full-year 1998 level. In 2008, China's two-way trade totaled US$2.56 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991 China joined the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation</a> group, a trade-promotion forum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2001, it also joined the World Trade Organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="border-bottom: 1px solid #ccc; background: transparent; text-align: left;">Theory</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist">

<li><a href="/wiki/International_trade#Ricardian_theory_of_international_trade" title="International trade">Ricardian trade theories</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Balassa%E2%80%93Samuelson_effect" title="Balassa–Samuelson effect">Balassa–Samuelson effect</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Linder_hypothesis" title="Linder hypothesis">Linder hypothesis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Leontief_paradox" title="Leontief paradox">Leontief paradox</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lerner_symmetry_theorem" title="Lerner symmetry theorem">Lerner symmetry theorem</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Terms_of_trade" title="Terms of trade">Terms of trade</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:World_trade" title="Template:World trade"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:World_trade" title="Template talk:World trade"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:World_trade&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>International trade is the exchange of goods and services across national borders.  In most countries, it represents a significant part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_Domestic_Product" title="Gross Domestic Product">GDP</a>. While international trade has been present throughout much of history (see Silk Road, <a href="/wiki/Amber_Road" title="Amber Road">Amber Road</a>), its economic, social, and political importance have increased in recent centuries, mainly because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">Industrialization</a>, advanced transportation, <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational corporations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Outsourcing" title="Outsourcing">outsourcing</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Empirical evidence for the success of trade can be seen in the contrast between countries such as <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, which adopted a policy of <a href="/wiki/Export-oriented_industrialization" title="Export-oriented industrialization">export-oriented industrialization</a>, and India, which historically had a more closed policy.  South Korea has done much better by economic criteria than India over the past fifty years, though its success also has to do with effective state institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_sanctions">Trade sanctions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Trade sanctions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_sanctions" title="Trade sanctions">Trade sanctions</a> against a specific country are sometimes imposed, in order to punish that country for some action.  An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embargo" title="Embargo">embargo</a>, a severe form of externally imposed isolation, is a blockade of all trade by one country on another. For example, the United States has had an <a href="/wiki/United_States_embargo_against_Cuba" title="United States embargo against Cuba">embargo</a> against <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> for over 40 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Embargoes are usually on a temporary basis. For example, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> put a temporary embargo on Turkish products and bans any imports from Turkey on December 31, 2020. The situation is prompted by food security concerns given Turkey's hostile attitude towards Armenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fair_trade">Fair trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fair trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The "<a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a>" movement, also known as the "trade justice" movement, promotes the use of <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">labour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">social</a> standards for the production of <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>, particularly those exported from the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_World" title="Second World">Second Worlds</a> to the <a href="/wiki/First_World" title="First World">First World</a>. Such ideas have also sparked a debate on whether trade itself should be codified as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_right" title="Human right">human right</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Importing firms voluntarily adhere to fair trade standards or governments may enforce them through a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Employment_law" title="Employment law">employment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commercial_law" title="Commercial law">commercial law</a>.  Proposed and practiced fair trade policies vary widely, ranging from the common prohibition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_(economics)" title="Good (economics)">goods</a> made using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_labour" title="Slave labour">slave labour</a> to minimum <a href="/wiki/Price_support" title="Price support">price support</a> schemes such as those for coffee in the 1980s. <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">Non-governmental organizations</a> also play a role in promoting fair trade standards by serving as independent monitors of compliance with labeling requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> As such, it is a form of Protectionism.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Trade&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1" id="CITEREFChisholm1911">Chisholm, Hugh, ed.
